    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1116258774" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1116258774" />
    <a title="Deea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8111258-deea"><img alt="Deea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525606324p2/8111258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1116258774?book_show_action=true">Nov 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deea" href="/user/show/8111258-deea">Deea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Deea by: </span>
      Dolors
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8111258-deea?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8111258-deea?shelf=best-2015">best-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8111258-deea?shelf=longlist-mbp">longlist-mbp</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1116258774" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10769856666748462699"><blockquote>
  <i>"The road was lined with magnificent homes dating back to the 1920s. Many had been demolished, but in the geography of my memory I saw them every day, entire, complete, standing proudly in a row. And in my memory I recalled the people who had lived there, who had passed through those homes; the scandals and the tragedies of their lives."</i>
</blockquote> For Philip Hutton, present is a scar of the past. In his old age, he can only open his eyes inward and relive his past, a past which has been witness to bot</span>
  <span id="freeText10769856666748462699" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>"The road was lined with magnificent homes dating back to the 1920s. Many had been demolished, but in the geography of my memory I saw them every day, entire, complete, standing proudly in a row. And in my memory I recalled the people who had lived there, who had passed through those homes; the scandals and the tragedies of their lives."</i>
</blockquote> For Philip Hutton, present is a scar of the past. In his old age, he can only open his eyes inward and relive his past, a past which has been witness to both great happiness and great loss for him. As an active and controversial participant of the World War II in Malaya, he was forced by circumstances to get involved in a whirlpool of events which took away his liberty of doing what was morally correct, but pushed him to do whatever it took to protect his family.<br /><br />Half European, half Chinese (having a British father and a Chinese mother), he feels he doesn't belong anywhere: both Europeans and Chinese reject him, considering him half-breed and not one of their kind. This is why he is bullied in school and feels he doesn't belong even in his family where all the other members are genuine British. He shares the religious beliefs of his father, so he adopts or knows more about Christianity than about the religious beliefs of his mother's people and he is reluctant at the beginning to believe in the Eastern belief that we are living countless lives trying to expiate sins from the past until we get to the higher state of being called Nirvana. <br /><blockquote>
  <i>
    <i>"Some mistakes can be so great, so grievous, that we end up paying for them again and again, all our lives until eventually we forget why we began paying in the first place".</i>
  </i>
</blockquote> When he meets Endo-san, his Japanese neighbor, he feels there is a strange connection between them. He becomes his pupil although the association with Japanese was not seen with good eyes and step by step he begins to realize that they are kindred spirits that have known each-other for several lives and have shared experiences that shaped the course of their current life. He is also reluctant to believe that our lives are predestined and no matter what decisions we make, the outcome is the same. It is hard for him to accept that the concept of free will is just an imagined attribute Western people think they have. In the end however, this belief is the only thing that helps him live within the three walls of his prison: anger, sorrow and guilt. It's this belief that helps him cope with the death of the members of his family: Isabel, his 2 brothers, his father. <blockquote>
  <i>
    <i>"To have the awareness that there is a greater power directing our destinies must give great comfort. It would give a sense of meaning to our lives, knowing that we are not running around vainly like mice in a maize".</i>
  </i>
</blockquote> His relation with Endo-san is one of a kind: it transcends history and it escapes time. They know each other so well that they can communicate their thoughts to each other without words as though they are part of the same brain. Their silences express a multitude of meanings to each other and can bring both relief and unthinkable grief. Philip learns from Endo-San to fight and to meditate and he models his strength through his lessons, his capacity of dealing with the world's hatred and love. He trusts him even when he hates him, he finds his strength in him, he accepts his betrayal and understands his motives before getting any explanation. He doubts his friend, but his love for him and his confidence in him is a lot stronger than his doubt and fortifies him to go on and on even when life seems to be devastating every bit of his spiritual power. He respects his friend's high sense of justice even when his actions are hard to digest.<br /><br />This book is an adventure: a quest in the culture of Japan and China and their relations with the British, the impact they had on World War II. It is so very intense that it keeps you hooked to its pages, it enraptures your mind and it activates your imagination. It spiritually enriched me in ways beyond my power of comprehension. I am so glad to have discovered it now as it totally resonated with me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10769856666748462699" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer55093506" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15024323455102280566">The setting is the island of Penang, off the coast of Malaya. The population is a complex mix of races, ideologies, and income levels. Chinese, Japanese, Malays, British, Indians, and many people of mixed race share this tiny island and have a history of tensions and race/class divisions. However, they've managed to find ways to live together in relative harmony.<br/><br/>When the Japanese occupy the island during World War II, concerns for personal and family safety lead to treachery and betrayal among t</span>
  <span id="freeText15024323455102280566" style="display:none">The setting is the island of Penang, off the coast of Malaya. The population is a complex mix of races, ideologies, and income levels. Chinese, Japanese, Malays, British, Indians, and many people of mixed race share this tiny island and have a history of tensions and race/class divisions. However, they've managed to find ways to live together in relative harmony.<br /><br />When the Japanese occupy the island during World War II, concerns for personal and family safety lead to treachery and betrayal among the inhabitants, even among friends. The racial and ideological divisions become more pronounced and cause people to turn on one another, with devastating results. <br /><br />By the end of the novel, you will no doubt feel that some of the characters made very wrong choices. But you'll also be hard pressed to say for sure what would have been better choices under the circumstances. The narrator, Philip Hutton, tells the story at the age of 75, looking back more than 50 years to the time of the events. He was barely more than a boy during the war, but he has never been able to forgive himself for the decisions he made and the heartache they caused.<br /><br />This book really made me think about gray areas and tough choices. We'd all like to think we would do the right thing in a difficult situation. But what if doing the right thing for your family means misery and death for many other innocent people? And what if trying to rectify your mistakes could potentially make things worse, and even lead to your own death? </span>
  <a data-text-id="15024323455102280566" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2956524100964766185" style="display:none">I know this book got some rave reviews, but about halfway thru I almost abandoned it. Which is odd because when I started it, I was fully engrossed and had that happy feeling of finding a book that I looked forward to nestling with and entering. I found the writing to be too flowery, and I also got bored. I did skim the rest of the book, which says alot since once I decide I'm bored I usually completely abandon it. <span id="freeTextContainer4909335185946882142">The year is 1939. Sixteen year old Philip Hutton is the youngest child of Noel Hutton, one of the wealthiest and most respected businessmen in Penang, Malaya. Noel was a widower with three Caucasian children when he met and married Philip’s Chinese born mother. She had died unexpectedly when Philip was very young.<br/><br/>Being a mixed race child, Philip had always felt alienated from his family and his schoolmates. When Noel planned a trip back to his native England, Philip chose not to go along with th</span>
  <span id="freeText4909335185946882142" style="display:none">The year is 1939. Sixteen year old Philip Hutton is the youngest child of Noel Hutton, one of the wealthiest and most respected businessmen in Penang, Malaya. Noel was a widower with three Caucasian children when he met and married Philip’s Chinese born mother. She had died unexpectedly when Philip was very young.<br /><br />Being a mixed race child, Philip had always felt alienated from his family and his schoolmates. When Noel planned a trip back to his native England, Philip chose not to go along with the rest of his family.<br /><br />While the family was vacationing, Philip began to feel isolated and alone. However, Philip’s feelings of loneliness begin to subside when he is befriended by Japanese diplomat, Hayato Endo. Philip proudly shows Mr. Endo all the sights around the island of Penang. In return, Mr. Endo offers to give Philip lessons in aikijutsu and ultimately becomes Philip’s sensei. <br /><br />As relations with Japan become strained, and rumors of war and invasion abound, Philip realizes that Mr. Endo is, in fact, a Japanese spy. Torn between his loyalty to his family and loyalty to his sensei, Philip makes a difficult choice which changes not only the course of his life, but also his family’s lives forever. <br /><br />This complex and intriguing novel of love, loyalty, honor, and betrayal will hold your interest until the very end.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15536945955319793341">I wish I could give this book more than five stars. It is the kind of book that reaches into your soul and leaves a scar there that will never disappear. A poignant and moving saga of choices, fates, destinies, struggles and regrets. Which of us has cannot look back and see moments that have separated us forever from others we love, times that our decisions cannot be understood and are too complicated to explain, choices that seem thrust upon us as if fate had all control and we had none. <br/><br/>Tan Tw</span>
  <span id="freeText15536945955319793341" style="display:none">I wish I could give this book more than five stars. It is the kind of book that reaches into your soul and leaves a scar there that will never disappear. A poignant and moving saga of choices, fates, destinies, struggles and regrets. Which of us has cannot look back and see moments that have separated us forever from others we love, times that our decisions cannot be understood and are too complicated to explain, choices that seem thrust upon us as if fate had all control and we had none. <br /><br />Tan Twan Eng is a masterful storyteller and weaves his magic around the heart and soul of who his characters are. I cannot imagine anyone who would not care about these people in a very personal and committed way. I clung to the book and yet tried to move slowly so that I would not miss a word and to allow my imagination to provide me with sensory images of these people and their world. <br /><br />If you have been disappointed lately with the authors you know (as has been my experience), this is an author that will shore you up and let you know that what you are looking for still exists. Take the ride. You will never regret it.</span>

<div id="review_3540120377" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3540120377" />
    <a title="Carol Orange" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9264803-carol-orange"><img alt="Carol Orange" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599700063p2/9264803.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3540120377?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol Orange" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol Orange" href="/user/show/9264803-carol-orange">Carol Orange</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3540120377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer950578196505793518">The Gift of Rain by Tan Twan Eng<br/><br/>In 1939 the lush Malayan island of Panang was part of the waning British Empire. The island’s residents included Chinese, Phillipinos, Indians, English businessmen, a sprinkling of Japanese and of course Malaysians. <br/><br/>Phillip Hutton, the protagonist, is the youngest son in a British family of successful businessman, Noel Hutton. Phillip’s mother, a beautiful Chinese woman died when he was young. Phillip was her only child. His two older brothers and sister were from</span>
  <span id="freeText950578196505793518" style="display:none">The Gift of Rain by Tan Twan Eng<br /><br />In 1939 the lush Malayan island of Panang was part of the waning British Empire. The island’s residents included Chinese, Phillipinos, Indians, English businessmen, a sprinkling of Japanese and of course Malaysians. <br /><br />Phillip Hutton, the protagonist, is the youngest son in a British family of successful businessman, Noel Hutton. Phillip’s mother, a beautiful Chinese woman died when he was young. Phillip was her only child. His two older brothers and sister were from Noel Hutton’s previous marriage to a British woman who died in childbirth. <br /><br />Early in 1939, when his family is in England for 6 months, Phillip who is now sixteen, takes a boat trip to a small island about a mile out from his family home. His family owns the island, but his father has rented the house on it to a Japanese man named Endo-san. Endo-san and Phillip connect and the story dramatically changes as the Japanese master of aikijutsu becomes Phillip’s martial arts teacher as well as his friend.<br /><br />Tan Twan Eng’s saga about the Malay peninsula is deeply moving as all the native worlds collide after the Japanese invade. Phillip, in his wish to protect his family, becomes an unwillingly traitor to his people while he secretly works to save as many lives as possible. <br /><br />This complex debut novel is written beautifully. It reveals a world that I knew nothing about. My heart was broken by all the tragic deaths, but Phillip survives and lives to tell the tale.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="950578196505793518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2218337725" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2218337725" />
    <a title="T.D. Whittle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6051061-t-d-whittle"><img alt="T.D. Whittle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532773499p2/6051061.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2218337725?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="T.D. Whittle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="T.D. Whittle" href="/user/show/6051061-t-d-whittle">T.D. Whittle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781905802142" href="/book/show/18489148-the-gift-of-rain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6051061-t-d-whittle?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6051061-t-d-whittle?shelf=tan-twan-eng">tan-twan-eng</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2218337725" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6107879540058533017">Here, at the eleventh hour, I am happy to declare <i>The Gift of Rain</i> my favourite read of 2017. (I'm excluding <i>War and Peace</i> because it seems unfair not to. I consider Tolstoy to occupy his own realm entirely.) I was utterly captivated by this book and so tense during the final third that my shoulders and back began to ache. I finished it at three o'clock this morning, and my sweet husband massaged the knots out of my body so that I could sleep. (I am not very practised at zazen, as you may have g</span>
  <span id="freeText6107879540058533017" style="display:none">Here, at the eleventh hour, I am happy to declare <i>The Gift of Rain</i> my favourite read of 2017. (I'm excluding <i>War and Peace</i> because it seems unfair not to. I consider Tolstoy to occupy his own realm entirely.) I was utterly captivated by this book and so tense during the final third that my shoulders and back began to ache. I finished it at three o'clock this morning, and my sweet husband massaged the knots out of my body so that I could sleep. (I am not very practised at zazen, as you may have guessed.) We have already reached the final day of 2017 here in Australia, and I am so pleased to have randomly selected this book to complete my year.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/439411060?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Samadrita's review</a> reflects my own thoughts and feelings so perfectly and eloquently, that I see no reason to add my own lengthy review. Just read hers and I will nod along saying, yes that's it, precisely. Yes, me too, me too. It is a book to cherish. Brutal and beautiful, all at the same time. <br /><br />My first book of 2018 is going to be <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12031532.The_Garden_of_Evening_Mists" title="The Garden of Evening Mists by Tan Twan Eng" rel="nofollow noopener">The Garden of Evening Mists</a> which is sitting here waiting for me, and I am already a bit disappointed that it looks half the length of the first. I seem to be enjoying long reads these days, even more so than usual.<br /><br />Happy New Year, fellow readers!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6107879540058533017" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1500068615" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1500068615" />
    <a title="Alice Poon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28700577-alice-poon"><img alt="Alice Poon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499099841p2/28700577.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1500068615?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alice Poon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alice Poon" href="/user/show/28700577-alice-poon">Alice Poon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28700577-alice-poon?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28700577-alice-poon?shelf=asia-themed">asia-themed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1500068615" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12899616466733723301"><br/>This was a very compelling read. The story is set in Penang, Malaysia, just before, during and shortly after the Japanese invasion and occupation in World War II. It follows the soulful trajectory of a half-Chinese, half-British local young lad, who learns the hard lessons of duty, love and loyalty in the midst of war-time brutalities, when it is most difficult to draw the line between right and wrong.<br/><br/>The book is divided into Part One and Part Two. Part One tells how Philip Hutton, a half-Britis</span>
  <span id="freeText12899616466733723301" style="display:none"><br />This was a very compelling read. The story is set in Penang, Malaysia, just before, during and shortly after the Japanese invasion and occupation in World War II. It follows the soulful trajectory of a half-Chinese, half-British local young lad, who learns the hard lessons of duty, love and loyalty in the midst of war-time brutalities, when it is most difficult to draw the line between right and wrong.<br /><br />The book is divided into Part One and Part Two. Part One tells how Philip Hutton, a half-British half-Chinese youngest son of a wealthy British merchant family meets with a Japanese consulate official, Endo-san, and how he comes to love and respect him as his sensei (teacher) in aikido and its attendant philosophy, Japanese culture and language, and the meanings of harmony and love. He is eager to show his Japanese sensei around Penang. Philip, whose Chinese mother died when he was a child, feels isolated from his British family members including his father, and finds affections and acceptance in Endo-san’s mentorship and friendship. This Part also deals with the cultural misunderstanding between Philip’s father and his maternal Chinese grandfather that caused great pain to his mother. After a meeting with the old man, who tells Philip the story of his youthful days as a tutor to a would-be Chinese emperor in the Qing court, the young man changes his perception of the old one and finds it in his heart to forgive his previous callousness towards his mother. Philip also comes to accept his father and half siblings.<br /><br />Part Two describes in the background the atrocities that the Japanese invaders inflict on the residents of Penang, and how Philip struggles with the dilemma between keeping his family safe and being an upright citizen. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[In his all-consuming desire to protect his family, he bows to Endo-san’s pressure and agrees to work for the Japanese. Now he realizes that Endo-san has been spying for the Japanese military and using him as a source of information. He finds himself an accomplice to the Japanese in executing innocent villagers and city residents. He also indirectly causes the death of his half sister and aunt. When his best friend Kon, who is with a British military camp, decides to engage in a dangerous plot against the Japanese, Philip becomes an informant in an effort to save Kon, but his effort failed. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The story is told through Philip’s recount of the events to a woman who comes from Japan and who wishes to know everything about Endo-san, having been in love with him. In the painful recalling and reliving of events, Philip at last finds peace with himself.<br /><br />I find the plot a complex and enthralling one, although a few details stretch the imagination a bit. The writing is lyrical and evocative of emotions and gives a beautiful description of the island of Penang.<br /><br />I’m giving it 4.3 stars.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12899616466733723301" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_53893569" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53893569" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/53893569?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=text-checked">text-checked</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=japan">japan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=kirkus">kirkus</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=malaysia">malaysia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer53893569" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7805592648301108463">NO SPOILERS<br/><br/>This book is clearly a mixed bag for me. It is in short about Philip, the son of a British father and Chinese mother, and his relationship with his Japanese sensei. It takes place in Malaya during WW2 when the Japanese occupied the country, sending the British running for their lives. The friendship between Philip and his older Japanese teacher, his sensei, begins before the occupation, before Philip ever became aware of the conflict of interests that will arise between the Malayan in</span>
  <span id="freeText7805592648301108463" style="display:none">NO SPOILERS<br /><br />This book is clearly a mixed bag for me. It is in short about Philip, the son of a British father and Chinese mother, and his relationship with his Japanese sensei. It takes place in Malaya during WW2 when the Japanese occupied the country, sending the British running for their lives. The friendship between Philip and his older Japanese teacher, his sensei, begins before the occupation, before Philip ever became aware of the conflict of interests that will arise between the Malayan inhabitants (Indians, Chinese and English living on the island of Penang) and the Japanese. The teaching begins with aikido training, but soon progresses to include an entire philosphy of life. Since Chinese, Japanese and Malayan people are represented in the community, the book is able to in depth illuminate the customs, traditions and philophies of the different groups. This is extremely well done. The history of the Japanese invasion is well explained. The reader isn't told, but experiences what this might have been like. The plot is moving and keeps you turning the page to find out what will happen next. <br /><br />I felt that the writing drew me in. I felt I was there. The ways of the people are cleverly interwoven into the storytelling. You are not given a text book rendition. I will site a few examples. When Philip first meets his grandfather, who had denied his existence since he opposed his daughter's marriage to a British man, proper etiquette had to be strictly adhered to:<br /><br />"On my first visit I had to sit across the table from him as etiquette decreed, but he had said, very irritably, 'No, no. Come and sit next to me.' And so after that I always sat by his side without being asked ...Then I would pour tea for him. The first time I had done so he had watched me fill the cup, rapping the second joints of the fore and middle fingers of his right hand softly on the table. It happened whenever I served him and eventually I asked him the significance of it." <br /><br />"'That is how we thank the person serving us,' he replied. 'All Chinese people are familiar with it.'" (page 243)<br /><br />And then the grandfather goes on to explain the origin of this custom. I found this fascinating. <br /><br />This is simply one example, but the book is chock full with such descriptions. Philip learn Japanese traditions from his sensei and Chinese ones from his grandfather. <br /><br />I like the philosophical questions that are raised. The Japanese sensei, Endo-san, is Philip's self-chosen teacher. As a teacher, the philosophical threads fit well into the narrative, but such discussions are not restricted to only these two. <br /><br />"That is what growing old consists of, mostly. One starts giving away items and belonings until only the memories are left. In the end, what else do we really require?"<br /><br />"I examined her words carefully, and the answer came slowly but without any equivocation. 'Someone to share the memories with,' I said finally surprising myself." (page 411)<br /><br />Or listen to the following:<br /><br />"The older one gets, the more one wishes to simplify one's life." (page 243)<br /><br />THAT is certainly true. <br /><br />Then there is a discussion between Philip and his half-sister Isabel about their mother. <br /><br />"She (Isabel) rolled her glass of wine between her hands, like a potter giving shape to his creation. 'Perhaps you're right. Memory is a tricky thing. When I said I have no memory of my mother, I meant I don't remember her here,' she touched her forehead, 'and yet-' her hands returned to shaping her glass. <br /><br />"'And yet you feel her here, I said resting my hand on the spot above my heart."<br /><br />"I stopped her moving hands and held them tight, feeling the harness of the wineglass beneath, almost on the point of cracking. 'That's not memory, Isabel,' I said. 'That's love.'" <br /><br />"She blinked her eyes again, and ran a finger across them, hiding her tears. We had revealed more about ourselves in those few moments than we had in recent years. Was this part of the process of growing up, that we finally noticed the people closest to us in a different, clearer light?" (pages 211-212)<br /><br />Thoughts kept my head churning. <br /><br />I must also note that the description of particular places was occasionally breathtaking. If it was raining, you felt the rain. The author drew little pictures in my mind that were utterly beautiful. Here is one example, when Philip, as an older man many years after the war, is in a little rowboat at night with a friend:<br /><br />"I dipped the oars into the water and slowed our progress. 'Close your eyes', I said. I switched off the flashlight and studied the movement of the clouds. The wind was pushing them across the weak moon, gradually filtering out its light from the sky, turning the night completely dark."<br /><br />"When we had drifted to the right place further down the river, I whispered softly, 'Now - open your eyes.'"<br /><br />"She drew in her breath. A light layer of mist rose up from the surface of the river and, in the trees, shining as though stars had fallen to earth, tens of thousands of fireflies were sending out their silent mating signals. We were caught in a frenzy of fragmented light."<br /><br />I don't know about you, but I think that is beautiful. Draw your own picture!<br /><br />The author's descriptive abilities are also used to describe savage Japanese torture procedures. <br /><br />So why only three stars? The sensei, Philips's father, grandfather and other relatives all believe that fate plays an all-important role in our lives. This is also a recurring theme of the entire book. This and other concepts of rebirth are a bit too metaphysical for my tastes. I simply cannot accept the idea proposed that our lives are ruled by fate. I do not believe we come back again as another identity. I cannot believe in the cyclical reappearnace of people. I do realize that this way of thinking IS strong in the East, but for me such beliefs only weakened the believability of the characters. The author failed to make this philosophy something I might understand. My inability to understand the way Philip and his sensei reason makes their choices seem absurd. I just don't find their actions believable. For me the portrayal of the two main characters falls flat. How can I feel empathy for cardboard, two-dimensional characters who have no pulse in their veins? Here the author has failed me. I think this book is overrated. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7805592648301108463" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3266040370" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3266040370" />
    <a title="Book Concierge" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1628041-book-concierge"><img alt="Book Concierge" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1324904606p2/1628041.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3266040370?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Book Concierge" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Book Concierge" href="/user/show/1628041-book-concierge">Book Concierge</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781602860247" href="/book/show/2440374.The_Gift_of_Rain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=concierge">concierge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=culture">culture</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=debut">debut</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=espionage">espionage</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=world-war-ii">world-war-ii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3266040370" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17958141117752994863">Historical fiction about the Pacific theater during World War II. Fifteen-year-old Patrick Hutton is the youngest child of a long-established British family with major industrial holdings in Malaya. His mother, however, was his father’s second wife, and Chinese; and he is shunned by both the Chinese community (for his British background and lifestyle), and by British society (for his Asian heritage). Lonely and adrift, he finds a friend in the Japanese diplomat who rents one of his family’s prop</span>
  <span id="freeText17958141117752994863" style="display:none">Historical fiction about the Pacific theater during World War II. Fifteen-year-old Patrick Hutton is the youngest child of a long-established British family with major industrial holdings in Malaya. His mother, however, was his father’s second wife, and Chinese; and he is shunned by both the Chinese community (for his British background and lifestyle), and by British society (for his Asian heritage). Lonely and adrift, he finds a friend in the Japanese diplomat who rents one of his family’s properties. Endo teaches Patrick the skills of akaido, and Patrick happily shares his love of his island home with this visitor. What he doesn’t realize until it is too late is that Endo is actually a Japanese spy, and that Patrick has unwittingly become complicit in helping the Japanese take over Penang and Malaya. <br /><br />This is a marvelous book on so many levels. First, the way in which these characters are drawn. They are complex and nuanced, and Eng manages to have the reader empathize with all sides of the story. Secondly, I applaud Eng for choosing a WW2 story that has had little exploration in fiction. I’ve read only two other books that touched on what happened in Malaya – <i>
  <b>The Narrow Road to the Deep North</b>
</i> by Flanagan, and Shute’s <i>
  <b>A Town Like Alice</b>
</i> - and both mostly mentioned the camps. This book really explained how the Japanese were able to take over the peninsula with little or no resistance from the British. <br /><br />Then there is the atmospheric nature of the book. I’ve been to Penang, and to Kuala Lumpur (the latter twice), but even if I had not experienced these locations Eng’s descriptions would easily have transported me there. I could feel the humidity, smell the cooking, relish in the feel of a sea breeze, hear the soft patter of a shower, the steady drumming of a monsoon, or the cacophony of a marketplace. And Eng’s prose is at times poetic, making me want to slow down and relish his use of language. And there were scenes where I was on the edge of my seat. <br /><br />This is Eng’s debut novel. I definitely will read more by him.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17958141117752994863" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1024965697" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1024965697" />
    <a title="Abby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6843326-abby"><img alt="Abby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1342372957p2/6843326.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1024965697?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abby" href="/user/show/6843326-abby">Abby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781602860742" href="/book/show/6325666-the-gift-of-rain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6843326-abby?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1024965697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4854912529599243156">Sometimes a good story, reasonably well told, is enough. And this is a hell of a good story in a beautifully evoked and exotic setting -- Malaya in 1939 and through the war. Philip Arminius Khoo-Hutton, a lonely sixteen-year-old whose Chinese mother died when he was a child, feels estranged from his prominent British colonial family and forms an ever-closer and more powerful connection with Hayato Endo, a Japanese diplomat and aikido master. Endo-san becomes Philip's sensei, both martial arts tr</span>
  <span id="freeText4854912529599243156" style="display:none">Sometimes a good story, reasonably well told, is enough. And this is a hell of a good story in a beautifully evoked and exotic setting -- Malaya in 1939 and through the war. Philip Arminius Khoo-Hutton, a lonely sixteen-year-old whose Chinese mother died when he was a child, feels estranged from his prominent British colonial family and forms an ever-closer and more powerful connection with Hayato Endo, a Japanese diplomat and aikido master. Endo-san becomes Philip's sensei, both martial arts trainer and spiritual guide. When war comes, the British abandon Malaya and Philip realizes he has been unwittingly complicit in the Japanese invasion and occupation. Conflicting loyalties to his family, his country and his mentor tear at Philip and drive both questionable and courageous decisions as his world falls apart.<br /><br />Tan Twan Eng's Malaya is a lush, seductive place, physically beautiful and rich with cultural cross-currents. His lyrical descriptive prose is often lovely. But people and their interactions are less deftly drawn, the dialog stilted and bordering on parody. (In one passage, all hungrily thrusting swords and Philip opening himself up to Endo-san, their relationship seemed to be going to a new place, never to be hinted at again.) As in many historical novels, the history is often clumsily presented. And there are too many schlocky fictional devices: wondering-if-I'd-ever-see-him-agains, b-movie action scenes, predestination from previous lives, hokey ancient wisdom.<br /><br />This is an ambitious saga -- a coming-of-age story and an exploration of collaboration and resistance in wartime -- maybe too ambitious for a debut novel. It's far from great literature but it's competent storytelling, a novel you can sink into, savor the richness of the sights and smells, and maybe even learn from.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4854912529599243156" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2167438617" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2167438617" />
    <a title="Jeanette" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11869072-jeanette"><img alt="Jeanette" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1372095557p2/11869072.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2167438617?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeanette" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeanette" href="/user/show/11869072-jeanette">Jeanette</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2167438617" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4283495732392006307">What to say!!!<br/><br/>Many post of the lyrical and exquisite balance of language, and the intense emotion of connections between Philip and his sensei. And the world of their island in Malaysia during this pre-war and WWII years. And the aftermath.<br/><br/>No plot tell here or synopsis. There's much action, it's not all poetic scenery and house parties. Book 1 is so well done that you felt that you were another set of eyes to Endo-san's island, and the lessons given there. But yet, I never lost that sense- other</span>
  <span id="freeText4283495732392006307" style="display:none">What to say!!!<br /><br />Many post of the lyrical and exquisite balance of language, and the intense emotion of connections between Philip and his sensei. And the world of their island in Malaysia during this pre-war and WWII years. And the aftermath.<br /><br />No plot tell here or synopsis. There's much action, it's not all poetic scenery and house parties. Book 1 is so well done that you felt that you were another set of eyes to Endo-san's island, and the lessons given there. But yet, I never lost that sense- others feel quite differently I see- that no 16 year old should ever be left in such material and spiritual choices with his family half a world away for 6 or more months. Let alone at that period of time when businessmen of such status and connections KNEW the lay of the land and had heard the sabers rattling for half a decade already. At least that long. No one mentions that in all of these reviews? The incredible naivete to put a child of mixed ancestry with his own servants in a place where bloodlines and family / tribal/ clan ties are 75% of all living and function! Especially for Philip's father- to be that dismissive of reality? <br /><br />Regardless, it is extremely long and the love stories expressed are coupled with attachment, mentoring of extreme physical and emotional connections, and the sense of "not a fit" or "different" or "not belonging" always at some point within the mix. Not only for Philip either. <br /><br />Book 2 and onwards until the much later day episodes with the two survivors that loved Endo-san is probably in the top 10 for all the saddest tales that I've ever read. And the entire great length is in some way an attempt to achieve the state of ambivalence for Endo-san and his choices and decisions for the "audience", the reader. And as such also Philip's which are so tied to his prime teacher and role model.<br /><br />And it seems to have been successful if I read the reviews and reactions here correctly. Most feel ambivalent. I never did. I feel more strongly than Isobel about the roles played. The past lives' roles, the family connection roles, the present day roles, and even the souls connections for the future. Brutal is brutal. Torture is torture. Evil of such whole piece belief system equals the products of its acts. Not only its words.<br /><br />But in this length and with so much substantiation of affect, this lonely boy and the life he experienced from 16 to 24! There was always some valid reason- some "good". And a predestined path to be walked. <br /><br />I'm with Philip's mother. This beautifully pictured cosmic scale? It is just a twisted and rusted metal contraption with humans consistently throwing in their own heavy thumbs on either side of the balance. <br /><br />Because I've read 4 or 5 books set in multi-language Malaysia this year- I have to add that the "Hill" houses and so much else of that changing world then and quite decades afterwards- that all was 5 star. Tan Twan Eng CAN write. I especially like how he posits all things in such gentle method wording. That's a gift. And it's never quite direct in language either, always building up or coming at you from "the sides".<br /><br />This is an extremely brutal and ultimately visceral account of Japanese occupation. Don't forget that if you decide to read this book. It is certainly not for everyone. And it was well done, but overlong. I enjoyed about 3/4th of it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4283495732392006307" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1425800252" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1425800252" />
    <a title="Palmyrah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2364523-palmyrah"><img alt="Palmyrah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1245049082p2/2364523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1425800252?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Palmyrah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Palmyrah" href="/user/show/2364523-palmyrah">Palmyrah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2364523-palmyrah?shelf=abandoned">abandoned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1425800252" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4020431070487190165"><b>Deliquescent.</b> Mr Tan starts out with a brilliant set of ingredients: the island of Penang immediately before and during the Second World War and the Japanese occupation; a Eurasian boy, just coming of age, who unlike most of his kind is from the highest ranks of both English and Chinese society; a mysterious Japanese diplomat who rents a small island belonging to the boy's father and proceeds to teach the boy Aikido (he is, of course, a Japanese advance agent and spy); the Straits of Malacca, a </span>
  <span id="freeText4020431070487190165" style="display:none"><b>Deliquescent.</b> Mr Tan starts out with a brilliant set of ingredients: the island of Penang immediately before and during the Second World War and the Japanese occupation; a Eurasian boy, just coming of age, who unlike most of his kind is from the highest ranks of both English and Chinese society; a mysterious Japanese diplomat who rents a small island belonging to the boy's father and proceeds to teach the boy Aikido (he is, of course, a Japanese advance agent and spy); the Straits of Malacca, a locale beloved of Conrad and Maugham and Theroux; tropical gardens and jungles, misty hillsides, exotic food and culture, strange Oriental faiths. It should be a recipe for dynamite.<br /><br />Instead we get a damp squib. And no wonder, because Mr Tan is as weepy as a girl. He's great on evoking the more conventionally exotic elements of Malaysia's multi-ethnic culture – a whiz at describing old Chinese temples and picturesque Penang houses. He excels at creating pretty little Merchant-Ivory images (handsome English boy going off to war in his sailor suit and the family Rolls, that kind of thing), but when he gets on to dialogue and character he's a drip. <br /><br />His drippiness is somewhat restrained in the first few chapters. His descriptions of the boy and his <i>sensei</i> at practice, and the martial-arts content in general, are workmanlike and quite vivid; apparently Mr Tan is some kind of Aikido master himself. But after a while his intially strong and elegant writing degenerates into something that resembles airline-magazine travelogue crossed with chick-lit, and there it stays. His hero is a sentimental young bore, much given to introspection of a rather pathetic sort, and forever melting with love for his father, his late mother, his grandfather, his siblings and above all for his <i>sensei</i>, for whom he seems to nurse a strong but unconscious homosexual crush (one which seems to be reciprocated, though as at page 278, where I stopped reading, all they'd done was beat each other up and then get misty at each other). It isn't at all clear that Mr Tan intends us to take his hero as gay, though since the story is about a youth on the verge of manhood and there are lots of personable men around him but no female interest in his life whatsoever, one is entitled draw one's own conclusons. <br /><br />It's a pity the author couldn't have been a little braver about this; then again, 'sodomy' is a crime in his country. But a frank acknowledgement of its hero's sexuality would have helped the book a lot. Perhaps there's a revelation at the end, I don't know. But what really makes <i>The Gift of Rain</i> a drag is that Mr Tan doesn't know how to tell a story, and his writing slowly collapses into flabby, cliché-strewn mess as the novel proceeds. <br /><br />Just before I gave up reading, the author had attempted a grand set-piece: a party thrown by the hero's father, who is one of the biggest English tycoons in Malaya, and to which all the active characters in the story so far are invited, along with most of the rest of Penang society. This is where the author's powers of description, so puissant when it comes to delineating marble fountains and places of worship with snakes in the rafters, should have been working overtime; instead, the party is given short shrift, turned into a hunt by our doughty heroes for a Man with a Bomb, who turns out, in true <i>Boy's Own Paper</i> style, to be a cowardly Indian Communist with greasy hair whom everyone happily beats up, to the evident approval of the author. At one point he does give us a brief look at the other guests diverting themselves (in the absence of their saboteur-hunting hosts) with a drunken brawl, which is broken up by the daughter of the house firing a gun into the air – at which the various diplomats, tycoons, newspaper editors and other pillars of society stop hitting each other and go home.<br /><br />I thought the real action (the Japanese invasion) would start after that — I was already more than halfway through the book — but insted the author took me strolling down Armenian Street to show me at yet another exquisite Penang house. I left him and his grandfather at the gate, and tiptoed away to write this review.<br /><br />Apparently this was longlisted for the 2007 Booker Prize. Must have been a bloody long list.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4020431070487190165" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2005080756" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2005080756" />
    <a title="DeB MaRtEnS" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9827967-deb-martens"><img alt="DeB MaRtEnS" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593294620p2/9827967.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2005080756?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="DeB MaRtEnS" class="user" itemprop="url" name="DeB MaRtEnS" href="/user/show/9827967-deb-martens">DeB MaRtEnS</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9827967-deb-martens?shelf=dramatic-fiction">dramatic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9827967-deb-martens?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9827967-deb-martens?shelf=africa-mideast-asia">africa-mideast-asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9827967-deb-martens?shelf=memorably-good">memorably-good</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9827967-deb-martens?shelf=war-stories">war-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2005080756" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17435882151929108307">I adored Tan Twan Eng’s second novel, “The Garden of the Evening Mists”. The language was so beautiful it made me ache at times. It is a huge story, intricately plotted, deeply emotional and one of the best books that I have ever read. <br/><br/>“The Gift of Rain” was nominated for a Booker Prize, and I expected to be swept along by it as I had been by Eng’s second novel. It does share a similar dream-like atmosphere, and at its historical centre are the atrocities committed by the Japanese during World W</span>
  <span id="freeText17435882151929108307" style="display:none">I adored Tan Twan Eng’s second novel, “The Garden of the Evening Mists”. The language was so beautiful it made me ache at times. It is a huge story, intricately plotted, deeply emotional and one of the best books that I have ever read. <br /><br />“The Gift of Rain” was nominated for a Booker Prize, and I expected to be swept along by it as I had been by Eng’s second novel. It does share a similar dream-like atmosphere, and at its historical centre are the atrocities committed by the Japanese during World War II in Malaysia. It too is rich with its descriptions of the Asian culture, those of Western origin who lived and loved the country and the conflicting feelings around the colonial empires that made fortunes through trade. <br /><br />Don’t get me wrong - “The Gift of Rain” is an exquisite novel. The writing is so good that it spoiled me for the next couple of books which came after. Eng is so talented, that it takes ones’ breath away... <br /><br />I’m a greedy soul, though, and not fair to Eng because I wanted to be transported in equal measure to “The Garden of the Evening Mists”, and I wasn’t quite. <br /><br />In the world of movies, this would be a Golden Globe. Darned excellent. Unfortunately, I experienced his second novel first - and it won an Oscar. <br /><br />“The Gift of Rain” is 4.5 stars... but I’ll rate it at four here, because, yes, I’m still measuring it against “The Garden of the Evening Mists”.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17435882151929108307" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zus3GlbiG5wOXFmYpb61Ni9rxTX+uydRh0/88hHnm9aUg3gdKl97gbSEv6sq4++m02GooCBHVHpurjBGBNkTtA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('j6ZR3aTFz4haYxggjsRHsFG3VK5+1HhWXguYsFaQ2+LVzh7a2HivleC7/hMBmR0grb05O6AoC3236lQEQ65TgA==')})
  }

Koji lives with the memories of Daniel’s mother, Mariko, the love of his life, and the scars of a life hard-lived. He can’t wait to see Daniel, who he’s always thought of as a son, but he knows the time has come to tell him the truth about his mother, and his father. But Daniel’s arrival coincides with the awakening of the Mauna Loa volcano, and its dangerous path toward their village stirs both new and long ago passions in their community.\n\nAlternating between past and present—from the day of the volcano eruption in 1935 to decades prior—The Color of Air interweaves the stories of Daniel, Koji, and Mariko to create a rich, vibrant, bittersweet chorus that celebrates their lifelong bond to one other and to their immigrant community.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/13847588.Tan_Twan_Eng">About Tan Twan Eng</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/13847588.Tan_Twan_Eng"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1500524463p3/13847588.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/13847588.Tan_Twan_Eng">Tan Twan Eng
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
600
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2574933264898789789">Tan Twan Eng was born in Penang and lived in various places in Malaysia as a child. He studied law at the University of London and later worked as lawyer in one of Kuala Lumpur’s most reputable law firms; in 2016, he was an International Writer-in-Residence at Nanyang Technological University in Singapore. Tan's first novel, <em>The Gift of Rain</em> (2007), was longlisted for the Man Booker Prize and has </span>
  <span id="freeText2574933264898789789" style="display:none">Tan Twan Eng was born in Penang and lived in various places in Malaysia as a child. He studied law at the University of London and later worked as lawyer in one of Kuala Lumpur’s most reputable law firms; in 2016, he was an International Writer-in-Residence at Nanyang Technological University in Singapore. Tan's first novel, <em>The Gift of Rain</em> (2007), was longlisted for the Man Booker Prize and has been translated into Italian, Spanish, Greek, Romanian, Czech and Serbian. <em>The Garden of Evening Mists</em> (2011), his second novel, won the Man Asian Literary Prize and Walter Scott Prize, and was shortlisted for the Man Booker Prize and the International IMPAC Dublin Literary Award.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2574933264898789789" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
